<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Geranium" title="Geranium">geranium</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 32</div><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption><span style="white-space:nowrap">Germanium,  <sub><span style="font-size:smaller;">32</span></sub>Ge</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polycrystalline-germanium.jpg" title="Grayish lustrous block with uneven cleaved surface"><img alt="Grayish lustrous block with uneven cleaved surface" data-file-height="1056" data-file-width="1056" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Polycrystalline-germanium.jpg/220px-Polycrystalline-germanium.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Polycrystalline-germanium.jpg/330px-Polycrystalline-germanium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Polycrystalline-germanium.jpg/440px-Polycrystalline-germanium.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#cccc99">General properties</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Pronunciation</th><td>
<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/dʒ/: 'j' in 'jam'">dʒ</span><span title="/ər/: 'er' in 'letter'">ər</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span></span>/</a></span></span> <wbr/>​<span class="nowrap">(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">jər-<span style="font-size:90%">MAY</span>-nee-əm</i></a>)</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Appearance</th><td>
grayish-white</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Standard_atomic_weight" title="Standard atomic weight">Standard atomic weight</a><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r865336399">.mw-parser-output .nobold{font-weight:normal}</style><span class="nobold"> (<i>A</i><sub>r, standard</sub>)</span></th><td>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_germanium" title="Template:Infobox germanium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_germanium" title="Template talk:Infobox germanium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_germanium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Germanium</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <i>Ge</i> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 32. It is a lustrous, hard, grayish-white <a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloid</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_group" title="Carbon group">carbon group</a>, chemically similar to its group neighbors <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>. Pure germanium is a <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a> with an appearance similar to elemental silicon. Like silicon, germanium naturally reacts and forms complexes with <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> in nature.
</p><p>Because it seldom appears in high concentration, germanium was discovered comparatively late in the history of chemistry. Germanium ranks near fiftieth <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth's crust">in relative abundance of the elements in the Earth's crust</a>. In 1869, <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev" title="Dmitri Mendeleev">Dmitri Mendeleev</a> <a href="/wiki/Mendeleev%27s_predicted_elements" title="Mendeleev's predicted elements">predicted</a> its existence and some of its properties from its position on his <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, and called the element <b><a href="/wiki/Mendeleev%27s_predicted_elements#Ekasilicon_and_germanium" title="Mendeleev's predicted elements">ekasilicon</a></b>. Nearly two decades later, in 1886, <a href="/wiki/Clemens_Winkler" title="Clemens Winkler">Clemens Winkler</a> found the new element along with <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, in a rare mineral called <a href="/wiki/Argyrodite" title="Argyrodite">argyrodite</a>. Although the new element somewhat resembled <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a> in appearance, the combining ratios in compounds agreed with Mendeleev's predictions for a relative of silicon. Winkler named the element after his country, <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Germany</a>. Today, germanium is mined primarily from <a href="/wiki/Sphalerite" title="Sphalerite">sphalerite</a> (the primary ore of zinc), though germanium is also recovered commercially from <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ores</a>.
</p><p>Elemental germanium is used as a semiconductor in <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistors</a> and various other electronic devices. Historically, the first decade of semiconductor electronics was based entirely on germanium. Today, the amount of germanium produced for semiconductor electronics is one fiftieth the amount of ultra-high purity silicon produced for the same. Presently, the major end uses are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fibre-optic" title="Fibre-optic">fibre-optic</a> systems, <a href="/wiki/Infrared_vision" title="Infrared vision">infrared optics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cell</a> applications, and <a href="/wiki/Light-emitting_diode" title="Light-emitting diode">light-emitting diodes</a> (LEDs). Germanium compounds are also used for <a href="/wiki/Polymerization" title="Polymerization">polymerization</a> catalysts and have most recently found use in the production of <a href="/wiki/Nanowire" title="Nanowire">nanowires</a>. This element forms a large number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organometallic" title="Organometallic">organometallic</a> compounds, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetraethylgermane" title="Tetraethylgermane">tetraethylgermane</a>, useful in <a href="/wiki/Organometallic_chemistry" title="Organometallic chemistry">organometallic chemistry</a>.
</p><p>Germanium is not thought to be an essential element for any living organism. Some complex organic germanium compounds are being investigated as possible pharmaceuticals, though none have yet proven successful. Similar to silicon and aluminum, natural germanium compounds tend to be insoluble in water and thus have little oral <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxicity</a>. However, synthetic soluble germanium salts are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nephrotoxic" title="Nephrotoxic">nephrotoxic</a>, and synthetic chemically reactive germanium compounds with <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> are irritants and toxins.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_transistor" title="History of the transistor">History of the transistor</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Winkler_preparate_1886_1904.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="755" data-file-width="1024" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Winkler_preparate_1886_1904.png/220px-Winkler_preparate_1886_1904.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Winkler_preparate_1886_1904.png/330px-Winkler_preparate_1886_1904.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Winkler_preparate_1886_1904.png/440px-Winkler_preparate_1886_1904.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Winkler_preparate_1886_1904.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Samples of germanium compounds prepared by <a href="/wiki/Clemens_Winkler" title="Clemens Winkler">Clemens Winkler</a>, discoverer of the element</div></div></div>
<p>In his report on <i>The Periodic Law of the Chemical Elements</i> in 1869, the Russian chemist Dmitri Ivanovich Mendeleev predicted the existence of several unknown <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a>, including one that would fill a gap in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Group_14_element" title="Group 14 element">carbon family</a> in his Periodic Table of the Elements, located between <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Because of its position in his Periodic Table, Mendeleev called it <i>ekasilicon (Es)</i>, and he estimated its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a> to be about 72.0.
</p><p>In mid-1885, at a mine near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freiberg,_Saxony" title="Freiberg, Saxony">Freiberg, Saxony</a>, a new <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">mineral</a> was discovered and named <i><a href="/wiki/Argyrodite" title="Argyrodite">argyrodite</a></i> because of the high <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[n 1]</a></sup> The chemist <a href="/wiki/Clemens_Winkler" title="Clemens Winkler">Clemens Winkler</a> analyzed this new mineral, which proved to be a combination of silver, sulfur, and a new element. Winkler was able to isolate the new element in 1886 and found it similar to <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>. He initially considered the new element to be eka-antimony, but was soon convinced that it was instead eka-silicon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winkle2_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Winkle2-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isolation_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-isolation-9">[8]</a></sup> Before Winkler published his results on the new element, he decided that he would name his element <i>neptunium</i>, since the recent discovery of planet <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> in 1846 had similarly been preceded by mathematical predictions of its existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[n 2]</a></sup> However, the name "neptunium" had already been given to another proposed chemical element (though not the element that today bears the name <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">neptunium</a>, which was discovered in 1940).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[n 3]</a></sup> So instead, Winkler named the new element <i>germanium</i> (from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word, <i>Germania</i>, for Germany) in honor of his homeland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isolation_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-isolation-9">[8]</a></sup> Argyrodite proved empirically to be Ag<sub>8</sub>GeS<sub>6</sub>.
</p><p>Because this new element showed some similarities with the elements <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> and antimony, its proper place in the periodic table was under consideration, but its similarities with Dmitri Mendeleev's predicted element "ekasilicon" confirmed that place on the periodic table.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isolation_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-isolation-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[15]</a></sup> With further material from 500 kg of ore from the mines in Saxony, Winkler confirmed the chemical properties of the new element in 1887.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winkle2_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Winkle2-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isolation_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-isolation-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[16]</a></sup> He also determined an atomic weight of 72.32 by analyzing pure <a href="/wiki/Germanium_tetrachloride" title="Germanium tetrachloride">germanium tetrachloride</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">GeCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>), while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lecoq_de_Boisbaudran" title="Lecoq de Boisbaudran">Lecoq de Boisbaudran</a> deduced 72.3 by a comparison of the lines in the spark <a href="/wiki/Spectrum" title="Spectrum">spectrum</a> of the element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Winkler was able to prepare several new compounds of germanium, including <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluorides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">chlorides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germanium_dioxide" title="Germanium dioxide">dioxide</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetraethylgermane" title="Tetraethylgermane">tetraethylgermane</a> (Ge(C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>4</sub>), the first organogermane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winkle2_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Winkle2-8">[7]</a></sup> The physical data from those compounds — which corresponded well with Mendeleev's predictions — made the discovery an important confirmation of Mendeleev's idea of element <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodicity</a>. Here is a comparison between the prediction and Winkler's data:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winkle2_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-Winkle2-8">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="float: center; margin: 5px;">

<p>Until the late 1930s, germanium was thought to be a poorly conducting <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOE_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-DOE-21">[18]</a></sup> Germanium did not become economically significant until after 1945 when its properties as an <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronic</a> semiconductor were recognized. During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, small amounts of germanium were used in some special <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronic devices</a>, mostly <a href="/wiki/Diode" title="Diode">diodes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup> The first major use was the point-contact <a href="/wiki/Schottky_diode" title="Schottky diode">Schottky diodes</a> for <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> pulse detection during the War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOE_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-DOE-21">[18]</a></sup> The first <a href="/wiki/Silicon-germanium" title="Silicon-germanium">silicon-germanium</a> alloys were obtained in 1955.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup> Before 1945, only a few hundred kilograms of germanium were produced in smelters each year, but by the end of the 1950s, the annual worldwide production had reached 40 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_ton" title="Metric ton">metric tons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acs_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-acs-25">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The development of the germanium <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistor</a> in 1948<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[23]</a></sup> opened the door to countless applications of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solid_state_(electronics)" title="Solid state (electronics)">solid state electronics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup> From 1950 through the early 1970s, this area provided an increasing market for germanium, but then high-purity silicon began replacing germanium in transistors, diodes, and <a href="/wiki/Rectifier" title="Rectifier">rectifiers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-28">[25]</a></sup> For example, the company that became <a href="/wiki/Fairchild_Semiconductor" title="Fairchild Semiconductor">Fairchild Semiconductor</a> was founded in 1957 with the express purpose of producing silicon transistors. Silicon has superior electrical properties, but it requires much greater purity that could not be commercially achieved in the early years of <a href="/wiki/Solid-state_electronics" title="Solid-state electronics">semiconductor electronics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, the demand for germanium for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fiber_optics" title="Fiber optics">fiber optic</a> communication networks, infrared <a href="/wiki/Night_vision" title="Night vision">night vision</a> systems, and <a href="/wiki/Polymerization" title="Polymerization">polymerization</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalysts" title="Catalysts">catalysts</a> increased dramatically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acs_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-acs-25">[22]</a></sup> These end uses represented 85% of worldwide germanium consumption in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-28">[25]</a></sup> The US government even designated germanium as a strategic and critical material, calling for a 146 <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">ton</a> (132 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">t</a>) supply in the national defense stockpile in 1987.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acs_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-acs-25">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Germanium differs from silicon in that the supply is limited by the availability of exploitable sources, while the supply of silicon is limited only by production capacity since silicon comes from ordinary sand and <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>. While silicon could be bought in 1998 for less than $10 per kg,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acs_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-acs-25">[22]</a></sup> the price of germanium was almost $800 per kg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acs_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-acs-25">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_conditions" title="Standard conditions">standard conditions</a>, germanium is a brittle, silvery-white, semi-metallic element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-30">[27]</a></sup> This form constitutes an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allotrope" title="Allotrope">allotrope</a> known as <i>α-germanium</i>, which has a metallic luster and a <a href="/wiki/Diamond_cubic" title="Diamond cubic">diamond cubic crystal structure</a>, the same as <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-28">[25]</a></sup> At pressures above 120 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">kbar</a>, it becomes the allotrope <i>β-germanium</i> with the same structure as β-<a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-31">[28]</a></sup> Like silicon, <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, germanium is one of the few substances that expands as it solidifies (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Freezing" title="Freezing">freezes</a>) from the molten state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-31">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Germanium is a <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zone_refining" title="Zone refining">Zone refining</a> techniques have led to the production of crystalline germanium for semiconductors that has an impurity of only one part in 10<sup>10</sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lanl_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-32">[29]</a></sup>
making it one of the purest materials ever obtained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup>
The first metallic material discovered (in 2005) to become a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superconductor" title="Superconductor">superconductor</a> in the presence of an extremely strong <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_field" title="Electromagnetic field">electromagnetic field</a> was an <a href="/wiki/Uranium_rhodium_germanium" title="Uranium rhodium germanium">alloy of germanium, uranium, and rhodium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pure germanium suffers from the forming of <a href="/wiki/Whisker_(metallurgy)" title="Whisker (metallurgy)">whiskers</a> by spontaneous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Screw_dislocation" title="Screw dislocation">screw dislocations</a>. If a whisker grows long enough to touch another part of the assembly or a metallic packaging, it can effectively <a href="/wiki/Shunt_(electrical)" title="Shunt (electrical)">shunt out</a> a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-n_junction" title="P-n junction">p-n junction</a>. This is one of the primary reasons for the failure of old germanium diodes and transistors.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Germanium_compounds" title="Category:Germanium compounds">Category:Germanium compounds</a>.</div>
<p>Elemental germanium oxidizes slowly to <a href="/wiki/Germanium_dioxide" title="Germanium dioxide">GeO<sub>2</sub></a> at 250 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup> Germanium is insoluble in dilute <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acids" title="Acids">acids</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkalis" title="Alkalis">alkalis</a> but dissolves slowly in hot concentrated sulfuric and nitric acids and reacts violently with molten alkalis to produce <a href="/wiki/Germanate" title="Germanate">germanates</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">[GeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>). Germanium occurs mostly in the <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> +4 although many +2 compounds are known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-36">[33]</a></sup> Other oxidation states are rare: +3 is found in compounds such as Ge<sub>2</sub>Cl<sub>6</sub>, and +3 and +1 are found on the surface of oxides,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[34]</a></sup> or negative oxidation states in <a href="/wiki/Germane" title="Germane">germanes</a>, such as −4 in <span class="chemf nowrap">GeH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>. Germanium cluster anions (<a href="/wiki/Zintl_phase" title="Zintl phase">Zintl</a> ions) such as Ge<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup>, Ge<sub>9</sub><sup>4−</sup>, Ge<sub>9</sub><sup>2−</sup>, [(Ge<sub>9</sub>)<sub>2</sub>]<sup>6−</sup> have been prepared by the extraction from alloys containing alkali metals and germanium in liquid ammonia in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Ethylenediamine" title="Ethylenediamine">ethylenediamine</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Cryptand" title="Cryptand">cryptand</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-36">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup> The oxidation states of the element in these ions are not integers—similar to the <a href="/wiki/Ozonide" title="Ozonide">ozonides</a> O<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>.
</p><p>Two <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a> of germanium are known: <a href="/wiki/Germanium_dioxide" title="Germanium dioxide">germanium dioxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">GeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, germania) and <a href="/wiki/Germanium_monoxide" title="Germanium monoxide">germanium monoxide</a>, (<span class="chemf nowrap">GeO</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-31">[28]</a></sup> The dioxide, GeO<sub>2</sub> can be obtained by roasting <a href="/wiki/Germanium_disulfide" title="Germanium disulfide">germanium disulfide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">GeS<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>), and is a white powder that is only slightly soluble in water but reacts with alkalis to form germanates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-31">[28]</a></sup> The monoxide, germanous oxide, can be obtained by the high temperature reaction of GeO<sub>2</sub> with Ge metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-31">[28]</a></sup> The dioxide (and the related oxides and germanates) exhibits the unusual property of having a high refractive index for visible light, but transparency to <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_germanate" title="Bismuth germanate">Bismuth germanate</a>, Bi<sub>4</sub>Ge<sub>3</sub>O<sub>12</sub>, (BGO) is used as a <a href="/wiki/Scintillator" title="Scintillator">scintillator</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BGO_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-BGO-41">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_compound" title="Binary compound">Binary compounds</a> with other <a href="/wiki/Chalcogen" title="Chalcogen">chalcogens</a> are also known, such as the di<a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">GeS<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>), di<a href="/wiki/Selenide" title="Selenide">selenide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">GeSe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>), and the <a href="/wiki/Germanium_monosulfide" title="Germanium monosulfide">monosulfide</a> (GeS), selenide (GeSe), and <a href="/wiki/Telluride_(chemistry)" title="Telluride (chemistry)">telluride</a> (GeTe).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-36">[33]</a></sup> GeS<sub>2</sub> forms as a white precipitate when hydrogen sulfide is passed through strongly acid solutions containing Ge(IV).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-36">[33]</a></sup> The disulfide is appreciably soluble in water and in solutions of caustic alkalis or alkaline sulfides. Nevertheless, it is not soluble in acidic water, which allowed Winkler to discover the element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup> By heating the disulfide in a current of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, the monosulfide (GeS) is formed, which sublimes in thin plates of a dark color and metallic luster, and is soluble in solutions of the caustic alkalis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_31-5"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-31">[28]</a></sup> Upon melting with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkali_metal_compound" title="Alkali metal compound">alkaline carbonates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, germanium compounds form salts known as thiogermanates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Germane-2D-dimensions.png"><img alt="Skeletal chemical structure of a tetrahedral molecule with germanium atom in its center bonded to four hydrogen atoms. The Ge-H distance is 152.51 picometers." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="889" data-file-width="1100" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Germane-2D-dimensions.png/170px-Germane-2D-dimensions.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Germane-2D-dimensions.png/255px-Germane-2D-dimensions.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Germane-2D-dimensions.png/340px-Germane-2D-dimensions.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Germane-2D-dimensions.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Germane is similar to <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Four tetra<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halides" title="Halides">halides</a> are known. Under normal conditions GeI<sub>4</sub> is a solid, GeF<sub>4</sub> a gas and the others volatile liquids. For example, <a href="/wiki/Germanium_tetrachloride" title="Germanium tetrachloride">germanium tetrachloride</a>, GeCl<sub>4</sub>, is obtained as a colorless fuming liquid boiling at 83.1 °C by heating the metal with chlorine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_31-6"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-31">[28]</a></sup> All the tetrahalides are readily hydrolyzed to hydrated germanium dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_31-7"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-31">[28]</a></sup> GeCl<sub>4</sub> is used in the production of organogermanium compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-36">[33]</a></sup> All four dihalides are known and in contrast to the tetrahalides are polymeric solids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood_36-5"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-36">[33]</a></sup> Additionally Ge<sub>2</sub>Cl<sub>6</sub> and some higher compounds of formula Ge<sub><i>n</i></sub>Cl<sub>2<i>n</i>+2</sub> are known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_31-8"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-31">[28]</a></sup> The unusual compound Ge<sub>6</sub>Cl<sub>16</sub> has been prepared that contains the Ge<sub>5</sub>Cl<sub>12</sub> unit with a <a href="/wiki/Neopentane" title="Neopentane">neopentane</a> structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Germane" title="Germane">Germane</a> (GeH<sub>4</sub>) is a compound similar in structure to <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>. Polygermanes—compounds that are similar to <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkanes</a>—with formula Ge<sub><i>n</i></sub>H<sub>2<i>n</i>+2</sub> containing up to five germanium atoms are known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood_36-6"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-36">[33]</a></sup> The germanes are less volatile and less reactive than their corresponding silicon analogues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood_36-7"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-36">[33]</a></sup> GeH<sub>4</sub> reacts with alkali metals in liquid ammonia to form white crystalline MGeH<sub>3</sub> which contain the GeH<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anion" title="Anion">anion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood_36-8"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-36">[33]</a></sup> The germanium hydrohalides with one, two and three halogen atoms are colorless reactive liquids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood_36-9"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-36">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NucleophilicAdditionWithOrganogermanium.png"><img alt="Skeletal chemical structures outlining an additive chemical reaction including an organogermanium compound." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="284" data-file-width="1022" height="61" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/NucleophilicAdditionWithOrganogermanium.png/220px-NucleophilicAdditionWithOrganogermanium.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/NucleophilicAdditionWithOrganogermanium.png/330px-NucleophilicAdditionWithOrganogermanium.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/NucleophilicAdditionWithOrganogermanium.png/440px-NucleophilicAdditionWithOrganogermanium.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NucleophilicAdditionWithOrganogermanium.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nucleophile" title="Nucleophile">Nucleophilic</a> addition with an organogermanium compound.</div></div></div>
<p>The first <a href="/wiki/Organogermanium_compound" title="Organogermanium compound">organogermanium compound</a> was synthesized by Winkler in 1887; the reaction of germanium tetrachloride with <a href="/wiki/Diethylzinc" title="Diethylzinc">diethylzinc</a> yielded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetraethylgermane" title="Tetraethylgermane">tetraethylgermane</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Ge(C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winkle2_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-Winkle2-8">[7]</a></sup> Organogermanes of the type R<sub>4</sub>Ge (where R is an <a href="/wiki/Alkyl" title="Alkyl">alkyl</a>) such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetramethylgermane" title="Tetramethylgermane">tetramethylgermane</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Ge(CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) and tetraethylgermane are accessed through the cheapest available germanium precursor <a href="/wiki/Germanium_tetrachloride" title="Germanium tetrachloride">germanium tetrachloride</a> and alkyl nucleophiles. Organic germanium hydrides such as <a href="/wiki/Isobutylgermane" title="Isobutylgermane">isobutylgermane</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">(CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CHCH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>GeH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) were found to be less hazardous and may be used as a liquid substitute for toxic <a href="/wiki/Germane" title="Germane">germane</a> gas in <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a> applications. Many germanium <a href="/wiki/Reactive_intermediate" title="Reactive intermediate">reactive intermediates</a> are known: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/-yl" title="-yl">germyl</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radical" title="Free radical">free radicals</a>, germylenes (similar to <a href="/wiki/Carbene" title="Carbene">carbenes</a>), and germynes (similar to <a href="/wiki/Carbyne" title="Carbyne">carbynes</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup> The organogermanium compound <a href="/wiki/Propagermanium" title="Propagermanium">2-carboxyethylgermasesquioxane</a> was first reported in the 1970s, and for a while was used as a dietary supplement and thought to possibly have anti-tumor qualities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toxic_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-toxic-47">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Using a ligand called Eind (1,1,3,3,5,5,7,7-octaethyl-s-hydrindacen-4-yl) germanium is able to form a double bond with oxygen (germanone).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_germanium" title="Isotopes of germanium">Isotopes of germanium</a></div>
<p>Germanium occurs in 5 natural <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>: <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">70</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ge<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">72</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ge<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">73</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ge<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">74</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ge<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, and <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">76</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ge<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>. Of these, <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">76</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ge<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> is very slightly radioactive, decaying by <a href="/wiki/Double_beta_decay" title="Double beta decay">double beta decay</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7028561725280000000♠</span>1.78<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>21</sup> years</span>. <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">74</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ge<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> is the most common isotope, having a <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">natural abundance</a> of approximately 36%. <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">76</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ge<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> is the least common with a natural abundance of approximately 7%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nubase_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-49">[46]</a></sup> When bombarded with alpha particles, the isotope <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">72</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ge<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> will generate stable <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selenium-77" title="Selenium-77"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">77</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Se<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>, releasing high energy electrons in the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72Ge_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-72Ge-50">[47]</a></sup> Because of this, it is used in combination with <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">radon</a> for <a href="/wiki/Atomic_battery" title="Atomic battery">nuclear batteries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72Ge_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-72Ge-50">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>At least 27 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotope" title="Radioisotope">radioisotopes</a> have also been synthesized, ranging in atomic mass from 58 to 89. The most stable of these is <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">68</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ge<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, decaying by <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> with a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7007234100800000000♠</span>270.95 d</span>ays. The least stable is <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">60</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ge<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, with a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6998300000000000000♠</span>30 <a href="/wiki/Millisecond" title="Millisecond">ms</a></span>. While most of germanium's radioisotopes decay by <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>, <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">61</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ge<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> and <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">64</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ge<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> decay by <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission" title="Positron emission"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>β<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a> delayed <a href="/wiki/Proton_emission" title="Proton emission">proton emission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nubase_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-49">[46]</a></sup> <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">84</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ge<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> through <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">87</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ge<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> isotopes also exhibit minor <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>β<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a> delayed <a href="/wiki/Neutron_emission" title="Neutron emission">neutron emission</a> decay paths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nubase_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-49">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Germanium_minerals" title="Category:Germanium minerals">Category:Germanium minerals</a>.</div>
<p>Germanium is created by <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a>, mostly by the <a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">s-process</a> in <a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_giant_branch" title="Asymptotic giant branch">asymptotic giant branch</a> stars. The s-process is a slow <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> capture of lighter elements inside pulsating <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a> stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sterling_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-sterling-51">[48]</a></sup> Germanium has been detected in some of the most distant stars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup> and in the atmosphere of Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Germanium's abundance <a href="/wiki/Earth#Chemical_composition" title="Earth">in the Earth's crust</a> is approximately 1.6 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holl_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holl-54">[51]</a></sup> Only a few minerals like <a href="/wiki/Argyrodite" title="Argyrodite">argyrodite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Briartite" title="Briartite">briartite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germanite" title="Germanite">germanite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Renierite" title="Renierite">renierite</a> contain appreciable amounts of germanium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-28">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup> Only few of them (especially germanite) are, very rarely, found in mineable amounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup> Some zinc-copper-lead ore bodies contain enough germanium to justify extraction from the final ore concentrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holl_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Holl-54">[51]</a></sup> An unusual natural enrichment process causes a high content of germanium in some coal seams, discovered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victor_Moritz_Goldschmidt" title="Victor Moritz Goldschmidt">Victor Moritz Goldschmidt</a> during a broad survey for germanium deposits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gold1_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gold1-59">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gold2_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gold2-60">[57]</a></sup> The highest concentration ever found was in <a href="/wiki/Hartley,_Northumberland" title="Hartley, Northumberland">Hartley</a> coal ash with as much as 1.6% germanium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gold1_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gold1-59">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gold2_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gold2-60">[57]</a></sup> The coal deposits near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xilinhaote" title="Xilinhaote">Xilinhaote</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a>, contain an estimated 1600 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> of germanium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holl_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-Holl-54">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Renierit.JPG"><img alt="A brown block of irregular shape and surface, about 6 cm in size." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="718" data-file-width="800" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Renierit.JPG/220px-Renierit.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Renierit.JPG/330px-Renierit.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Renierit.JPG/440px-Renierit.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Renierit.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Renierite" title="Renierite">Renierite</a></div></div></div>
<p>About 118 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> of germanium was produced in 2011 worldwide, mostly in China (80 t), Russia (5 t) and United States (3 t).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-28">[25]</a></sup> Germanium is recovered as a by-product from <a href="/wiki/Sphalerite" title="Sphalerite">sphalerite</a> <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> ores where it is concentrated in amounts as great as 0.3%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup> especially from low-temperature sediment-hosted, massive <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">Zn</a>–<a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">Pb</a>–<a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">Cu</a>(–<a href="/wiki/Barium" title="Barium">Ba</a>) deposits and carbonate-hosted Zn–Pb deposits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup> A recent study found that at least 10,000 t of extractable germanium is contained in known zinc reserves, particularly those hosted by <a href="/wiki/Carbonate-hosted_lead-zinc_ore_deposits" title="Carbonate-hosted lead-zinc ore deposits">Mississippi-Valley type deposits</a>, while at least 112,000 t will be found in coal reserves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup> In 2007 35% of the demand was met by recycled germanium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holl_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-Holl-54">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>While it is produced mainly from <a href="/wiki/Sphalerite" title="Sphalerite">sphalerite</a>, it is also found in <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> ores. Another source of germanium is <a href="/wiki/Fly_ash" title="Fly ash">fly ash</a> of power plants fueled from coal deposits that contain germanium. Russia and China used this as a source for germanium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naumov_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naumov-65">[62]</a></sup> Russia's deposits are located in the far east of <a href="/wiki/Sakhalin" title="Sakhalin">Sakhalin</a> Island, and northeast of <a href="/wiki/Vladivostok" title="Vladivostok">Vladivostok</a>. The deposits in China are located mainly in the <a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">lignite</a> mines near <a href="/wiki/Lincang" title="Lincang">Lincang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a>; coal is also mined near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xilinhaote" title="Xilinhaote">Xilinhaote</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holl_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-Holl-54">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="float: right; margin: 5px;">
<table class="wikitable">
