<p>The ore concentrates are mostly <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfidic</a>; they are converted to the <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a> by heating under air in a process known as <a href="/wiki/Roasting_(metallurgy)" title="Roasting (metallurgy)">roasting</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>GeS<sub>2</sub> + 3 O<sub>2</sub> → GeO<sub>2</sub> + 2 SO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Some of the germanium is left in the dust produced, while the rest is converted to germanates, which are then leached (together with zinc) from the cinder by sulfuric acid. After neutralization, only the zinc stays in solution while germanium and other metals precipitate. After removing some of the zinc in the precipitate by the <a href="/wiki/Waelz_process" title="Waelz process">Waelz process</a>, the residing Waelz oxide is leached a second time. The <a href="/wiki/Germanium_dioxide" title="Germanium dioxide">dioxide</a> is obtained as precipitate and converted with <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> gas or hydrochloric acid to <a href="/wiki/Germanium_tetrachloride" title="Germanium tetrachloride">germanium tetrachloride</a>, which has a low boiling point and can be isolated by distillation:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naumov_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Naumov-65">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>GeO<sub>2</sub> + 4 HCl → GeCl<sub>4</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd>
<dd>GeO<sub>2</sub> + 2 Cl<sub>2</sub> → GeCl<sub>4</sub> + O<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Germanium tetrachloride is either hydrolyzed to the oxide (GeO<sub>2</sub>) or purified by fractional distillation and then hydrolyzed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naumov_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-Naumov-65">[62]</a></sup> The highly pure GeO<sub>2</sub> is now suitable for the production of germanium glass. It is reduced to the element by reacting it with hydrogen, producing germanium suitable for infrared optics and semiconductor production:
</p>
<dl><dd>GeO<sub>2</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub> → Ge + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>The germanium for steel production and other industrial processes is normally reduced using carbon:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moska_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moska-67">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>GeO<sub>2</sub> + C → Ge + CO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Singlemode_fibre_structure.svg"><img alt="A drawing of four concentric cylinders." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="378" data-file-width="419" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Singlemode_fibre_structure.svg/220px-Singlemode_fibre_structure.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Singlemode_fibre_structure.svg/330px-Singlemode_fibre_structure.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Singlemode_fibre_structure.svg/440px-Singlemode_fibre_structure.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Singlemode_fibre_structure.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical single-mode optical fiber. Germanium oxide is a <a href="/wiki/Dopant" title="Dopant">dopant</a> of the core silica (Item 1).<br/> 1. Core 8 µm<br/> 2. Cladding 125 µm<br/> 3. Buffer 250 µm<br/> 4. Jacket 400 µm</div></div></div>
<p>The major end uses for germanium in 2007, worldwide, were estimated to be: 35% for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fiber-optic" title="Fiber-optic">fiber-optics</a>, 30% <a href="/wiki/Infrared_vision" title="Infrared vision">infrared optics</a>, 15% <a href="/wiki/Polymerization" title="Polymerization">polymerization</a> catalysts, and 15% electronics and solar electric applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-28">[25]</a></sup> The remaining 5% went into such uses as phosphors, metallurgy, and chemotherapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_28-6"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-28">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Optics">Optics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Optics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The notable properties of <a href="/wiki/Germanium_dioxide" title="Germanium dioxide">germania</a> (GeO<sub>2</sub>) are its high <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">index of refraction</a> and its low <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(optics)" title="Dispersion (optics)">optical dispersion</a>. These make it especially useful for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wide-angle_camera_lens" title="Wide-angle camera lens">wide-angle camera lenses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microscopy" title="Microscopy">microscopy</a>, and the core part of <a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber" title="Optical fiber">optical fibers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-69">[66]</a></sup> It has replaced <a href="/wiki/Titanium_dioxide" title="Titanium dioxide">titania</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Dopant" title="Dopant">dopant</a> for silica fiber, eliminating the subsequent heat treatment that made the fibers brittle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup> At the end of 2002, the fiber optics industry consumed 60% of the annual germanium use in the United States, but this is less than 10% of worldwide consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-69">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/GeSbTe" title="GeSbTe">GeSbTe</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phase_change_material" title="Phase change material">phase change material</a> used for its optic properties, such as that used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DVD-RW" title="DVD-RW">rewritable DVDs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because germanium is transparent in the infrared wavelengths, it is an important <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> optical material that can be readily cut and polished into lenses and windows. It is especially used as the front optic in <a href="/wiki/Thermographic_camera" title="Thermographic camera">thermal imaging cameras</a> working in the 8 to 14 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">micron</a> range for passive thermal imaging and for hot-spot detection in military, mobile <a href="/wiki/Night_vision" title="Night vision">night vision</a>, and fire fighting applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moska_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moska-67">[64]</a></sup> It is used in infrared <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectroscope" title="Spectroscope">spectroscopes</a> and other optical equipment that require extremely sensitive <a href="/wiki/Infrared_photography" title="Infrared photography">infrared detectors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-69">[66]</a></sup> It has a very high <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> (4.0) and must be coated with anti-reflection agents. Particularly, a very hard special antireflection coating of <a href="/wiki/Diamond-like_carbon" title="Diamond-like carbon">diamond-like carbon</a> (DLC), refractive index 2.0, is a good match and produces a diamond-hard surface that can withstand much environmental abuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electronics">Electronics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Electronics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Silicon-germanium" title="Silicon-germanium">Silicon-germanium</a> alloys are rapidly becoming an important semiconductor material for high-speed integrated circuits. Circuits utilizing the properties of Si-SiGe junctions can be much faster than those using silicon alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup> Silicon-germanium is beginning to replace <a href="/wiki/Gallium_arsenide" title="Gallium arsenide">gallium arsenide</a> (GaAs) in wireless communications devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_28-7"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-28">[25]</a></sup> The SiGe chips, with high-speed properties, can be made with low-cost, well-established production techniques of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicon_chip" title="Silicon chip">silicon chip</a> industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_28-8"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-28">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">Solar panels</a> are a major use of germanium. Germanium is the substrate of the wafers for high-efficiency <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multijunction_photovoltaic_cell" title="Multijunction photovoltaic cell">multijunction photovoltaic cells</a> for space applications. High-brightness LEDs, used for automobile headlights and to backlight LCD screens, are an important application.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_28-9"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-28">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because germanium and <a href="/wiki/Gallium_arsenide" title="Gallium arsenide">gallium arsenide</a> have very similar lattice constants, germanium substrates can be used to make gallium arsenide <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mars_Exploration_Rover" title="Mars Exploration Rover">Mars Exploration Rovers</a> and several satellites use triple junction gallium arsenide on germanium cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Germanium-on-insulator substrates are seen as a potential replacement for silicon on miniaturized chips.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_28-10"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-28">[25]</a></sup> Other uses in electronics include <a href="/wiki/Phosphor" title="Phosphor">phosphors</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">fluorescent lamps</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lanl_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-32">[29]</a></sup> and solid-state light-emitting diodes (LEDs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_28-11"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-28">[25]</a></sup> Germanium transistors are still used in some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effects_pedal" title="Effects pedal">effects pedals</a> by musicians who wish to reproduce the distinctive tonal character of the <a href="/wiki/Distortion_(music)" title="Distortion (music)">"fuzz"-tone</a> from the early <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">rock and roll</a> era, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Fuzz_Face" title="Fuzz Face">Dallas Arbiter Fuzz Face</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses">Other uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Other uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pet_Flasche.JPG"><img alt="Photo of a standard transparent plastic bottle." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3008" data-file-width="2000" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Pet_Flasche.JPG/170px-Pet_Flasche.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Pet_Flasche.JPG/255px-Pet_Flasche.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Pet_Flasche.JPG/340px-Pet_Flasche.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pet_Flasche.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene_terephthalate" title="Polyethylene terephthalate">PET</a> <a href="/wiki/Bottle" title="Bottle">bottle</a></div></div></div>
<p>Germanium dioxide is also used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalysts</a> for <a href="/wiki/Polymerization" title="Polymerization">polymerization</a> in the production of <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene_terephthalate" title="Polyethylene terephthalate">polyethylene terephthalate</a> (PET).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thiele_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thiele-78">[75]</a></sup> The high brilliance of this polyester is especially favored for PET bottles marketed in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thiele_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thiele-78">[75]</a></sup> In the United States, germanium is not used for polymerization catalysts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_28-12"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-28">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to the similarity between silica (SiO<sub>2</sub>) and germanium dioxide (GeO<sub>2</sub>), the silica stationary phase in some <a href="/wiki/Gas_chromatography" title="Gas chromatography">gas chromatography</a> columns can be replaced by GeO<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>In recent years germanium has seen increasing use in precious metal alloys. In <a href="/wiki/Sterling_silver" title="Sterling silver">sterling silver</a> alloys, for instance, it reduces <a href="/wiki/Firescale" title="Firescale">firescale</a>, increases tarnish resistance, and improves precipitation hardening. A tarnish-proof silver alloy trademarked <a href="/wiki/Argentium_sterling_silver" title="Argentium sterling silver">Argentium</a> contains 1.2% germanium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_28-13"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-28">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_detector#Germanium_detector" title="Semiconductor detector">Semiconductor detectors</a> made of single crystal high-purity germanium can precisely identify radiation sources—for example in airport security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup> Germanium is useful for <a href="/wiki/Crystal_monochromator" title="Crystal monochromator">monochromators</a> for <a href="/wiki/Beamline" title="Beamline">beamlines</a> used in <a href="/wiki/Single_crystal" title="Single crystal">single crystal</a> <a href="/wiki/Neutron_scattering" title="Neutron scattering">neutron scattering</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synchrotron_light" title="Synchrotron light">synchrotron X-ray</a> diffraction. The reflectivity has advantages over silicon in neutron and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_energy_X-rays" title="High energy X-rays">high energy X-ray</a> applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup> Crystals of high purity germanium are used in detectors for <a href="/wiki/Gamma_spectroscopy" title="Gamma spectroscopy">gamma spectroscopy</a> and the search for <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup> Germanium crystals are also used in X-ray spectrometers for the determination of phosphorus, chlorine and sulfur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Germanium is emerging as an important material for <a href="/wiki/Spintronics" title="Spintronics">spintronics</a> and spin-based <a href="/wiki/Quantum_computing" title="Quantum computing">quantum computing</a> applications. In 2010, researchers demonstrated room temperature spin transport <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup> and more recently donor electron spins in germanium has been shown to have very long <a href="/wiki/Coherence_time" title="Coherence time">coherence times</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germanium_and_health">Germanium and health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Germanium and health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Germanium is not considered essential to the health of plants or animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-American_Cancer_Society_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-American_Cancer_Society-86">[83]</a></sup> Germanium in the environment has little or no health impact.  This is primarily because it usually occurs only as a trace element in ores and <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbonaceous</a> materials, and the various industrial and electronic applications involve very small quantities that are not likely to be ingested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_28-14"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-28">[25]</a></sup> For similar reasons, end-use germanium has little impact on the environment as a biohazard. Some reactive intermediate compounds of germanium are poisonous (see precautions, below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_Jr_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_Jr-87">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Germanium supplements, made from both organic and inorganic germanium, have been marketed as an <a href="/wiki/Alternative_medicine" title="Alternative medicine">alternative medicine</a> capable of treating <a href="/wiki/Leukemia" title="Leukemia">leukemia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acs_25-5"><a href="#cite_note-acs-25">[22]</a></sup> There is, however, no <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">medical evidence</a> of benefit; some evidence suggests that such supplements are actively harmful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-American_Cancer_Society_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-American_Cancer_Society-86">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some germanium compounds have been administered by alternative medical practitioners as non-FDA-allowed injectable solutions. Soluble inorganic forms of germanium used at first, notably the citrate-lactate salt, resulted in some cases of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renal" title="Renal">renal</a> dysfunction, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hepatic_steatosis" title="Hepatic steatosis">hepatic steatosis</a>, and peripheral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neuropathy" title="Neuropathy">neuropathy</a> in individuals using them over a long term. Plasma and urine germanium concentrations in these individuals, several of whom died, were several orders of magnitude greater than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endogenous" title="Endogenous">endogenous</a> levels. A more recent organic form, beta-carboxyethylgermanium sesquioxide (<a href="/wiki/Propagermanium" title="Propagermanium">propagermanium</a>), has not exhibited the same spectrum of toxic effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="U.S. Food and Drug Administration">U.S. Food and Drug Administration</a> research has concluded that inorganic germanium, when used as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nutritional_supplement" title="Nutritional supplement">nutritional supplement</a>, "presents potential human <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Health_hazard" title="Health hazard">health hazard</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toxic_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-toxic-47">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Certain compounds of germanium have low toxicity to <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, but have toxic effects against certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacterium" title="Bacterium">bacteria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-30">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions_for_chemically_reactive_germanium_compounds">Precautions for chemically reactive germanium compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Precautions for chemically reactive germanium compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some of germanium's artificially-produced compounds are quite reactive and present an immediate hazard to human health on exposure. For example, <a href="/wiki/Germanium_tetrachloride" title="Germanium tetrachloride">germanium chloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germane" title="Germane">germane</a> (GeH<sub>4</sub>) are a liquid and gas, respectively, that can be very irritating to the eyes, skin, lungs, and throat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerber_1997_141–146_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerber_1997_141–146-89">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Future">Future</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Germanium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Future">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As of the year 2000, about 15% of <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> consumption of germanium was used for infrared optics technology and 50% for fiber-optics. Over the past 20 years, infrared use has consistently decreased; fiber optic demand, however, is slowly increasing. In America, 30–50% of current fiber optic lines are unused <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dark_fiber" title="Dark fiber">dark fiber</a>, sparking discussion of over-production and a future reduction in demand. Worldwide, demand is increasing dramatically as countries such as China are installing fiber optic telecommunication lines throughout the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
